<p>The <b>mallard</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;m&#39; in &#39;my&#39;">m</span><span title="/æ/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;bad&#39;">æ</span><span title="&#39;l&#39; in &#39;lie&#39;">l</span><span title="/ɑːr/: &#39;ar&#39; in &#39;far&#39;">ɑːr</span><span title="&#39;d&#39; in &#39;dye&#39;">d</span></span>,<span class="wrap"> </span><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;m&#39; in &#39;my&#39;">m</span><span title="/æ/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;bad&#39;">æ</span><span title="&#39;l&#39; in &#39;lie&#39;">l</span><span title="/ər/: &#39;er&#39; in &#39;letter&#39;">ər</span><span title="&#39;d&#39; in &#39;dye&#39;">d</span></span>/</a></span></span>) (<i>Anas platyrhynchos</i>) is a <a href="/wiki/Dabbling_duck" class="mw-redirect" title="Dabbling duck">dabbling duck</a> that breeds throughout the temperate and subtropical Americas, <a href="/wiki/Euro-Siberian_region" title="Euro-Siberian region">Eurosiberia</a>, and North Africa and has been <a href="/wiki/Introduced_species" title="Introduced species">introduced</a> to New Zealand, Australia, Peru, Brazil, Uruguay, Argentina, Chile, Colombia, the Falkland Islands, and South Africa. This <a href="/wiki/Duck" title="Duck">duck</a> belongs to the <a href="/wiki/Subfamily" title="Subfamily">subfamily</a> <a href="/wiki/Anatinae" title="Anatinae">Anatinae</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Anseriformes" title="Anseriformes">waterfowl</a> family <a href="/wiki/Anatidae" title="Anatidae">Anatidae</a>. The male birds (drakes) have a glossy green head and are grey on their wings and belly, while the females (hens or ducks) have mainly brown-speckled <a href="/wiki/Plumage" title="Plumage">plumage</a>. Both sexes have an area of white-bordered black or iridescent blue feathers called a <a href="/wiki/Speculum_feathers" title="Speculum feathers">speculum</a> on their wings; males especially tend to have blue speculum feathers. The mallard is 50–65&#160;cm (20–26&#160;in) long, of which the body makes up around two-thirds the length. The wingspan is 81–98&#160;cm (32–39&#160;in) and the <a href="/wiki/Beak" title="Beak">bill</a> is 4.4 to 6.1&#160;cm (1.7 to 2.4&#160;in) long. It is often slightly heavier than most other dabbling ducks, weighing 0.72–1.58&#160;kg (1.6–3.5&#160;lb). Mallards live in <a href="/wiki/Wetland" title="Wetland">wetlands</a>, eat water plants and small animals, and are <a href="/wiki/Social_animal" class="mw-redirect" title="Social animal">social animals</a> preferring to congregate in groups or flocks of varying sizes. This species is the main ancestor of most breeds of <a href="/wiki/Domestic_duck" title="Domestic duck">domesticated ducks</a>.
</p><p>The female lays eight to thirteen creamy white to greenish-buff spotless <a href="/wiki/Bird_egg" title="Bird egg">eggs</a>, on alternate days. <a href="/wiki/Egg_incubation" title="Egg incubation">Incubation</a> takes 27 to 28 days and <a href="/wiki/Fledge" title="Fledge">fledging</a> takes 50 to 60 days. The ducklings are <a href="/wiki/Precocial" class="mw-redirect" title="Precocial">precocial</a> and fully capable of swimming as soon as they hatch.
</p><p>The mallard is considered to be a <a href="/wiki/Species_of_least_concern" class="mw-redirect" title="Species of least concern">species of least concern</a> by the <a href="/wiki/International_Union_for_Conservation_of_Nature" title="International Union for Conservation of Nature">International Union for Conservation of Nature</a> (IUCN). Unlike many waterfowl, mallards are considered an <a href="/wiki/Invasive_species" title="Invasive species">invasive species</a> in some regions. It is a very adaptable species, being able to live and even thrive in urban areas which may have supported more localised, sensitive species of waterfowl before development. The non-migratory mallard interbreeds with indigenous wild ducks of closely related species through <a href="/wiki/Genetic_pollution" title="Genetic pollution">genetic pollution</a> by producing fertile offspring. Complete hybridisation of various species of wild duck gene pools could result in the extinction of many indigenous waterfowl. The wild mallard is the ancestor of most domestic ducks, and its naturally evolved wild gene pool gets genetically polluted by the domesticated and feral mallard populations.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy_and_evolutionary_history">Taxonomy and evolutionary history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mallard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Taxonomy and evolutionary history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Anas_Rubripes_and_Anas_Platyrhynchos_August_2008.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Anas_Rubripes_and_Anas_Platyrhynchos_August_2008.JPG/220px-Anas_Rubripes_and_Anas_Platyrhynchos_August_2008.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="135" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Anas_Rubripes_and_Anas_Platyrhynchos_August_2008.JPG/330px-Anas_Rubripes_and_Anas_Platyrhynchos_August_2008.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Anas_Rubripes_and_Anas_Platyrhynchos_August_2008.JPG/440px-Anas_Rubripes_and_Anas_Platyrhynchos_August_2008.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2656" data-file-height="1628" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Anas_Rubripes_and_Anas_Platyrhynchos_August_2008.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An <a href="/wiki/American_black_duck" title="American black duck">American black duck</a> (top left) and a male mallard (bottom right) in eclipse plumage</div></div></div>
<p>The mallard was one of the many <a href="/wiki/Aves_in_the_10th_edition_of_Systema_Naturae" title="Aves in the 10th edition of Systema Naturae">bird species originally described</a> in the 1758 10th<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>edition of <i><a href="/wiki/Systema_Naturae" title="Systema Naturae">Systema Naturae</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Carl Linnaeus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Linnaeus1758_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Linnaeus1758-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> He gave it two <a href="/wiki/Binomial_nomenclature" title="Binomial nomenclature">binomial names</a>: <i>Anas platyrhynchos</i> and <i>Anas boschas</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The latter was generally preferred until 1906 when <a href="/wiki/Einar_L%C3%B6nnberg" title="Einar Lönnberg">Einar Lönnberg</a> established that <i>A.<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>platyrhynchos</i> had priority, as it appeared on an earlier page in the text.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The scientific name comes from <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i>Anas</i>, "duck" and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a> πλατυρυγχος, <i>platyrhynchus</i>, "broad-billed" (from πλατύς, <i>platys</i>, "broad" and ρυγχός, <i>rhunkhos</i>, "bill").<sup id="cite_ref-job90_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-job90-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genome</a> of <i>Anas platyrhynchos</i> was sequenced in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-Huang2013_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Huang2013-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The name <i>Mallard</i> originally referred to any wild drake, and it is sometimes still used this way.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> It was derived from the <a href="/wiki/Old_French" title="Old French">Old French</a> <i><i lang="fr" title="French language text">malart</i></i> or <i><i lang="fr" title="French language text">mallart</i></i> for "wild drake" although its true derivation is unclear.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> It may be related to, or at least influenced by, an <a href="/wiki/Old_High_German" title="Old High German">Old High German</a> masculine proper name <i><i lang="de" title="German language text">Madelhart</i></i>, clues lying in the alternate English forms "maudelard" or "mawdelard".<sup id="cite_ref-OED2_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OED2-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> <i>Masle</i> (male) has also been proposed as an influence.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Mallards frequently interbreed with their closest relatives in the <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Anas" title="Anas">Anas</a></i>, such as the <a href="/wiki/American_black_duck" title="American black duck">American black duck</a>, and also with species more distantly related, such as the <a href="/wiki/Northern_pintail" title="Northern pintail">northern pintail</a>, leading to various <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">hybrids</a> that may be fully fertile.<sup id="cite_ref-Phillips_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> This is quite unusual among such different species, and is apparently because the mallard <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolved</a> very rapidly and recently, during the <a href="/wiki/Late_Pleistocene" title="Late Pleistocene">Late Pleistocene</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> The distinct lineages of this radiation are usually kept separate due to non-overlapping ranges and behavioural cues, but have not yet reached the point where they are fully genetically incompatible.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Mallards and their domesticated <a href="/wiki/Conspecific" class="mw-redirect" title="Conspecific">conspecifics</a> are also fully interfertile.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Genetic analysis has shown that certain mallards appear to be closer to their <a href="/wiki/Indo-Pacific" title="Indo-Pacific">Indo-Pacific</a> relatives while others are related to their American relatives.<sup id="cite_ref-johnson_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-johnson-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">Mitochondrial DNA</a> data for the <a href="/wiki/D-loop_replication" title="D-loop replication">D-loop sequence</a> suggests that mallards may have evolved in the general area of <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a>. Mallard bones rather abruptly appear in food remains of ancient humans and other deposits of <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossil</a> bones in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, without a good candidate for a local predecessor species.<sup id="cite_ref-Kulikova_et_al._2005_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kulikova_et_al._2005-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> The large <a href="/wiki/Ice_Age" class="mw-redirect" title="Ice Age">Ice Age</a> <a href="/wiki/Chronospecies" title="Chronospecies">palaeosubspecies</a> that made up at least the European and west Asian populations during the <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a> has been named <i>Anas platyrhynchos palaeoboschas</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Mallards are differentiated in their mitochondrial DNA between North American and Eurasian populations,<sup id="cite_ref-Kraus2011_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kraus2011-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> but the nuclear genome displays a notable lack of genetic structure.<sup id="cite_ref-Kraus2013_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kraus2013-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Haplotypes" class="mw-redirect" title="Haplotypes">Haplotypes</a> typical of American mallard relatives and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_spot-billed_duck" title="Eastern spot-billed duck">Eastern spot-billed ducks</a> can be found in mallards around the <a href="/wiki/Bering_Sea" title="Bering Sea">Bering Sea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Kulikova_et_al._2004_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kulikova_et_al._2004-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Aleutian_Islands" title="Aleutian Islands">Aleutian Islands</a> hold a population of mallards that appear to be evolving towards a <a href="/wiki/Subspecies" title="Subspecies">subspecies</a>, as <a href="/wiki/Gene_flow" title="Gene flow">gene flow</a> with other populations is very limited.<sup id="cite_ref-Kulikova_et_al._2005_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kulikova_et_al._2005-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Also, the paucity of <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">morphological</a> differences between the <a href="/wiki/Old_World" title="Old World">Old World</a> mallards and the <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a> mallard demonstrates the extent to which the genome is shared among them such that birds like the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_spot-billed_duck" class="mw-redirect" title="Chinese spot-billed duck">Chinese spot-billed duck</a> are highly similar to the Old World mallard, and birds such as the Hawaiian duck are highly similar to the New World mallard.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The size of the mallard varies <a href="/wiki/Cline_(biology)" title="Cline (biology)">clinally</a>; for example, birds from <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a>, though larger, have smaller bills, paler plumage, and stockier bodies than birds further south, and are sometimes classified as a separate subspecies, the Greenland mallard (<i>A.<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>p.<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>conboschas</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-:11_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:11-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mallard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Description">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Anas_platyrhynchos_juvenile_JdP_2013-06-14_n01_retusche.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Anas_platyrhynchos_juvenile_JdP_2013-06-14_n01_retusche.jpg/220px-Anas_platyrhynchos_juvenile_JdP_2013-06-14_n01_retusche.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="101" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Anas_platyrhynchos_juvenile_JdP_2013-06-14_n01_retusche.jpg/330px-Anas_platyrhynchos_juvenile_JdP_2013-06-14_n01_retusche.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Anas_platyrhynchos_juvenile_JdP_2013-06-14_n01_retusche.jpg/440px-Anas_platyrhynchos_juvenile_JdP_2013-06-14_n01_retusche.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2858" data-file-height="1306" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Anas_platyrhynchos_juvenile_JdP_2013-06-14_n01_retusche.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Juvenile male and female</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mallard_speculum.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Mallard_speculum.jpg/170px-Mallard_speculum.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="204" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Mallard_speculum.jpg/255px-Mallard_speculum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Mallard_speculum.jpg/340px-Mallard_speculum.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1331" data-file-height="1600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mallard_speculum.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Iridescent <a href="/wiki/Speculum_feathers" title="Speculum feathers">speculum feathers</a> of the male</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_1" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="35.383922902494" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Mallard_(Anas_platyrhynchos)_(W1CDR0001518_BD17).ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/9/95/Mallard_%28Anas_platyrhynchos%29_%28W1CDR0001518_BD17%29.ogg/Mallard_%28Anas_platyrhynchos%29_%28W1CDR0001518_BD17%29.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="132032" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/95/Mallard_%28Anas_platyrhynchos%29_%28W1CDR0001518_BD17%29.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (211 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="211240" /></audio></div>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mallard_(Anas_platyrhynchos)_(W1CDR0001518_BD17).ogg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A group of mallards quacking</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mallard_Duckling.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Mallard_Duckling.jpg/220px-Mallard_Duckling.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Mallard_Duckling.jpg/330px-Mallard_Duckling.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Mallard_Duckling.jpg/440px-Mallard_Duckling.jpg 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="643" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mallard_Duckling.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Duckling</div></div></div>
<p>The mallard is a medium-sized waterfowl species that is often slightly heavier than most other dabbling ducks. It is 50–65&#160;cm (20–26&#160;in) long&#160;&#8211;&#32;of which the body makes up around two-thirds&#160;&#8211;&#32;has a wingspan of 81–98&#160;cm (32–39&#160;in),<sup id="cite_ref-cramp_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:<span>505</span></sup> and weighs 0.72–1.58&#160;kg (1.6–3.5&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Among standard measurements, the <a href="/wiki/Wing_chord_(biology)" title="Wing chord (biology)">wing chord</a> is 25.7 to 30.6&#160;cm (10.1 to 12.0&#160;in), the <a href="/wiki/Beak" title="Beak">bill</a> is 4.4 to 6.1&#160;cm (1.7 to 2.4&#160;in), and the <a href="/wiki/Tarsus_(skeleton)" title="Tarsus (skeleton)">tarsus</a> is 4.1 to 4.8&#160;cm (1.6 to 1.9&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Madge_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Madge-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The breeding male mallard is unmistakable, with a glossy bottle-green head and a white collar that demarcates the head from the purple-tinged brown breast, grey-brown wings, and a pale grey belly.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The rear of the male is black, with white-bordered dark tail feathers.<sup id="cite_ref-cramp_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:<span>506</span></sup> The bill of the male is a yellowish-orange tipped with black, with that of the female generally darker and ranging from black to mottled orange and brown.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The female mallard is predominantly mottled, with each individual feather showing sharp contrast from buff to very dark brown, a coloration shared by most female dabbling ducks, and has buff cheeks, eyebrow, throat, and neck, with a darker crown and eye-stripe.<sup id="cite_ref-cramp_22-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:<span>506</span></sup>
</p><p>Both male and female mallards have distinct iridescent purple-blue speculum feathers edged with white, which are prominent in flight or at rest but temporarily shed during the annual summer moult.<sup id="cite_ref-:7_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Upon hatching, the plumage of the duckling is yellow on the underside and face (with streaks by the eyes) and black on the back (with some yellow spots) all the way to the top and back of the head.<sup id="cite_ref-:16_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:16-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Its legs and bill are also black.<sup id="cite_ref-:16_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:16-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> As it nears a month in age, the duckling's plumage starts becoming drab, looking more like the female, though more streaked, and its legs lose their dark grey colouring.<sup id="cite_ref-cramp_22-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:<span>506</span></sup> Two months after hatching, the fledgling period has ended, and the duckling is now a juvenile.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Between three and four months of age, the juvenile can finally begin flying, as its wings are fully developed for flight (which can be confirmed by the sight of purple speculum feathers). Its bill soon loses its dark grey colouring, and its sex can finally be distinguished visually by three factors: 1)<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>the bill is yellow in males, but black and orange in females;<sup id="cite_ref-:12_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:12-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Self-published_sources" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on a self-published source. (December 2017)">self-published source</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> 2)<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>the breast feathers are reddish-brown in males, but brown in females;<sup id="cite_ref-:12_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:12-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> and 3)<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>in males, the centre tail feather (drake feather) is curled, but in females, the centre tail feather is straight.<sup id="cite_ref-:12_30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:12-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> During the final period of maturity leading up to adulthood (6–10 months of age), the plumage of female juveniles remains the same while the plumage of male juveniles gradually changes to its characteristic colours.<sup id="cite_ref-:13_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:13-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> This change in plumage also applies to adult mallard males when they transition in and out of their non-breeding <a href="/wiki/Eclipse_plumage" class="mw-redirect" title="Eclipse plumage">eclipse plumage</a> at the beginning and the end of the summer moulting period.<sup id="cite_ref-:13_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:13-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> The adulthood age for mallards is fourteen months, and the average life expectancy is three years, but they can live to twenty.<sup id="cite_ref-BTO_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BTO-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Several species of duck have brown-plumaged females that can be confused with the female mallard.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> The female <a href="/wiki/Gadwall" title="Gadwall">gadwall</a> (<i>Mareca strepera</i>) has an orange-lined bill, white belly, black and white speculum that is seen as a white square on the wings in flight, and is a smaller bird.<sup id="cite_ref-cramp_22-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:<span>506</span></sup> More similar to the female mallard in <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a> are the American black duck (<i>A.<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>rubripes</i>), which is notably darker-hued in both sexes than the mallard,<sup id="cite_ref-:17_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:17-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Mottled_duck" title="Mottled duck">mottled duck</a> (<i>A.<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>fulvigula</i>), which is somewhat darker than the female mallard, and with slightly different bare-part colouration and no white edge on the speculum.<sup id="cite_ref-:17_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:17-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In captivity, domestic ducks come in wild-type plumages, white, and other colours.<sup id="cite_ref-:18_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:18-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Most of these colour variants are also known in domestic mallards not bred as livestock, but kept as pets, <a href="/wiki/Aviary" title="Aviary">aviary</a> birds, etc., where they are rare but increasing in availability.<sup id="cite_ref-:18_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:18-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Female_Mallard_with_faded_plumage_at_Bray_Lock,_May_2015.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/34/Female_Mallard_with_faded_plumage_at_Bray_Lock%2C_May_2015.JPG/220px-Female_Mallard_with_faded_plumage_at_Bray_Lock%2C_May_2015.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/34/Female_Mallard_with_faded_plumage_at_Bray_Lock%2C_May_2015.JPG/330px-Female_Mallard_with_faded_plumage_at_Bray_Lock%2C_May_2015.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/34/Female_Mallard_with_faded_plumage_at_Bray_Lock%2C_May_2015.JPG/440px-Female_Mallard_with_faded_plumage_at_Bray_Lock%2C_May_2015.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4896" data-file-height="3672" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Female_Mallard_with_faded_plumage_at_Bray_Lock,_May_2015.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Owing to their highly 'malleable' genetic code, mallards can display a large amount of variation,<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> as seen here with this female, who displays faded or 'apricot' plumage.</div></div></div><p>A noisy species, the female has the deep <i>quack</i> stereotypically associated with ducks.<sup id="cite_ref-cramp_22-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:<span>507</span></sup> Male mallards make a sound phonetically similar to that of the female, a typical <i>quack</i>, but it is deeper and quieter compared to that of the female. When <a href="/wiki/Egg_incubation" title="Egg incubation">incubating</a> a nest, or when offspring are present, females vocalise differently, making a call that sounds like a truncated version of the usual quack. This maternal vocalization is highly attractive to their young. The repetition and <a href="/wiki/Frequency" title="Frequency">frequency modulation</a> of these <i>quacks</i> form the auditory basis for <a href="/wiki/Intra-species_recognition" title="Intra-species recognition">species identification</a> in offspring, a process known as acoustic <a href="/wiki/Conspecific" class="mw-redirect" title="Conspecific">conspecific</a> identification.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> In addition, females hiss if the nest or offspring are threatened or interfered with. When taking off, the wings of a mallard produce a characteristic faint whistling noise.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The mallard is a rare example of both <a href="/wiki/Allen%27s_Rule" class="mw-redirect" title="Allen&#39;s Rule">Allen's Rule</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bergmann%27s_Rule" class="mw-redirect" title="Bergmann&#39;s Rule">Bergmann's Rule</a> in birds.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Bergmann's Rule, which states that polar forms tend to be larger than related ones from warmer climates, has numerous examples in birds,<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> as in case of the Greenland mallard which is larger than the mallards further south.<sup id="cite_ref-:11_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:11-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Allen's Rule says that appendages like ears tend to be smaller in polar forms to minimise heat loss, and larger in tropical and desert equivalents to facilitate heat diffusion, and that the polar taxa are stockier overall.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Examples of this rule in birds are rare as they lack external ears, but the bill of ducks is supplied with a few blood vessels to prevent heat loss,<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> and, as in the Greenland mallard, the bill is smaller than that of birds farther south, illustrating the rule.<sup id="cite_ref-:11_21-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:11-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Due to the variability of the mallard's genetic code, which gives it its vast interbreeding capability, mutations in the genes that decide plumage colour are very common and have resulted in a wide variety of hybrids such as <a href="/wiki/Brewer%27s_duck" title="Brewer&#39;s duck">Brewer's duck</a> (mallard × gadwall, <i>Mareca strepera</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Audubon_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Audubon-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution_and_habitat">Distribution and habitat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mallard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Distribution and habitat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Birds_of_Sweden_2016_52.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Birds_of_Sweden_2016_52.jpg/220px-Birds_of_Sweden_2016_52.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Birds_of_Sweden_2016_52.jpg/330px-Birds_of_Sweden_2016_52.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Birds_of_Sweden_2016_52.jpg/440px-Birds_of_Sweden_2016_52.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3883" data-file-height="2912" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Birds_of_Sweden_2016_52.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Male mallard</div></div></div><p>The mallard is widely distributed across the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere" title="Northern Hemisphere">Northern</a> and <a href="/wiki/Southern_Hemisphere" title="Southern Hemisphere">Southern</a> Hemispheres; in North America its range extends from southern and central Alaska to Mexico, the <a href="/wiki/Hawaiian_Islands" title="Hawaiian Islands">Hawaiian Islands</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-:1_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> across the <a href="/wiki/Palearctic" class="mw-redirect" title="Palearctic">Palearctic</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> from Iceland<sup id="cite_ref-:2_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> and southern Greenland<sup id="cite_ref-:1_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> and parts of Morocco (<a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-:2_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> in the west, <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a><sup id="cite_ref-:2_46-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> and Britain<sup id="cite_ref-:2_46-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> to the north, and to Siberia,<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Japan,<sup id="cite_ref-Yamaguchi_875–881_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yamaguchi_875–881-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> and South Korea,<sup id="cite_ref-Yamaguchi_875–881_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yamaguchi_875–881-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>. Also in the east, it ranges to south-eastern and south-western Australia<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> and New Zealand<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> in the Southern hemisphere.<sup id="cite_ref-cramp_22-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:<span>505</span></sup><sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> It is strongly <a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">migratory</a> in the northern parts of its breeding range, and winters farther south.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> For example, in North America, it winters south to the <a href="/wiki/Southern_United_States" title="Southern United States">southern United States</a> and <a href="/wiki/Northern_Mexico" title="Northern Mexico">northern Mexico</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> but also regularly strays into <a href="/wiki/Central_America" title="Central America">Central America</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a> between September and May.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> A drake later named "Trevor" attracted media attention in 2018 when it turned up on the island of <a href="/wiki/Niue" title="Niue">Niue</a>, an atypical location for mallards.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The mallard inhabits a wide range of habitats and climates, from Arctic tundra to subtropical regions.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> It is found in both fresh- and salt-water wetlands, including parks, small ponds, rivers, lakes and <a href="/wiki/Estuary" title="Estuary">estuaries</a>, as well as shallow inlets and open sea within sight of the coastline.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Water depths of less than 0.9 metres (3.0&#160;ft) are preferred, with birds avoiding areas more than a few metres deep.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> They are attracted to bodies of water with aquatic vegetation.<sup id="cite_ref-cramp_22-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:<span>507</span></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Behaviour">Behaviour</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mallard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Behaviour">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Feeding">Feeding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mallard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Feeding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The mallard is omnivorous and very flexible in its choice of food.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Its diet may vary based on several factors, including the stage of the breeding cycle, short-term variations in available food, <a href="/wiki/Nutrient" title="Nutrient">nutrient</a> availability, and <a href="/wiki/Interspecific_competition" title="Interspecific competition">interspecific</a> and <a href="/wiki/Intraspecific_competition" title="Intraspecific competition">intraspecific competition</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Krapu_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Krapu-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> The majority of the mallard's diet seems to be made up of <a href="/wiki/Gastropod" class="mw-redirect" title="Gastropod">gastropods</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-:14_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:14-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrates</a> (including beetles, flies, <a href="/wiki/Lepidoptera" title="Lepidoptera">lepidopterans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dragonfly" title="Dragonfly">dragonflies</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Caddisfly" title="Caddisfly">caddisflies</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Crustacean" title="Crustacean">crustaceans</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-:15_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:15-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> worms,<sup id="cite_ref-:14_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:14-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> many varieties of seeds and plant matter,<sup id="cite_ref-:14_63-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:14-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> and roots and <a href="/wiki/Tubers" class="mw-redirect" title="Tubers">tubers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:15_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:15-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> During the breeding season, male birds were recorded to have eaten 37.6% animal matter and 62.4% plant matter, most notably the grass <i><a href="/wiki/Echinochloa_crus-galli" title="Echinochloa crus-galli">Echinochloa crus-galli</a></i>, and nonlaying females ate 37.0% animal matter and 63.0% plant matter, while laying females ate 71.9% animal matter and only 28.1% plant matter.<sup id="cite_ref-Swanson_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swanson-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Plants generally make up the larger part of a bird's diet, especially during autumn migration and in the winter.<sup id="cite_ref-Gruenhagen_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gruenhagen-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Combs_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Combs-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The mallard usually feeds by dabbling for plant food or grazing; there are reports of it eating <a href="/wiki/Frog" title="Frog">frogs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:6_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> However, in 2017 a flock of mallards in Romania were observed hunting small migratory birds, including <a href="/wiki/Grey_wagtail" title="Grey wagtail">grey wagtail</a> and <a href="/wiki/Black_redstart" title="Black redstart">black redstart</a>, the first documented occasion they had been seen attacking and consuming large vertebrates.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> It usually nests on a river bank, but not always near water. It is highly gregarious outside of the breeding season and forms large <a href="/wiki/Flock_(birds)" title="Flock (birds)">flocks</a>, which are known as "sordes."<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Breeding">Breeding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mallard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Breeding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

data-height=&quot;360&quot; data-bandwidth=&quot;961472&quot; data-framerate=&quot;180018.002&quot;/&gt;&lt;source src=&quot;//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/0/02/Canard_colvert_femelle_et_ses_petits_%28Lac_Archambault%2C_Qc%29.webm/Canard_colvert_femelle_et_ses_petits_%28Lac_Archambault%2C_Qc%29.webm.360p.webm&quot; type=&quot;video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;&quot; data-title=&quot;WebM (360P)&quot; data-shorttitle=&quot;WebM 360P&quot; data-transcodekey=&quot;360p.webm&quot; data-width=&quot;640&quot; data-height=&quot;360&quot; data-bandwidth=&quot;1607560&quot; data-framerate=&quot;180018.002&quot;/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;"><img alt="File:Canard colvert femelle et ses petits (Lac Archambault, Qc).webm" style="width:220px;height:124px" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Canard_colvert_femelle_et_ses_petits_%28Lac_Archambault%2C_Qc%29.webm/220px--Canard_colvert_femelle_et_ses_petits_%28Lac_Archambault%2C_Qc%29.webm.jpg" /><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/02/Canard_colvert_femelle_et_ses_petits_%28Lac_Archambault%2C_Qc%29.webm" title="Play media" target="new"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Canard_colvert_femelle_et_ses_petits_(Lac_Archambault,_Qc).webm" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Female Mallard with five ducklings</div></div></div><p>
Mallards usually form pairs (in October and November in the Northern Hemisphere) until the female lays eggs at the start of the nesting season, which is around the beginning of spring.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> At this time she is left by the male who joins up with other males to await the moulting period, which begins in June (in the Northern Hemisphere).<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> During the brief time before this, however, the males are still sexually potent and some of them either remain on standby to sire replacement <a href="/wiki/Clutch_(eggs)" title="Clutch (eggs)">clutches</a> (for female mallards that have lost or abandoned their previous clutch)<sup id="cite_ref-:20_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:20-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> or forcibly mate with females that appear to be isolated or unattached regardless of their species and whether or not they have a brood of ducklings.<sup id="cite_ref-:20_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:20-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Anas_platyrhynchos_MWNH_1048.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Anas_platyrhynchos_MWNH_1048.JPG/220px-Anas_platyrhynchos_MWNH_1048.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Anas_platyrhynchos_MWNH_1048.JPG/330px-Anas_platyrhynchos_MWNH_1048.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Anas_platyrhynchos_MWNH_1048.JPG/440px-Anas_platyrhynchos_MWNH_1048.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2921" data-file-height="1831" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Anas_platyrhynchos_MWNH_1048.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Egg, Collection <a href="/wiki/Museum_Wiesbaden" title="Museum Wiesbaden">Museum Wiesbaden</a></div></div></div>
<p>Egg clutches number 8–13 creamy white to greenish-buff eggs free of speckles.<sup id="cite_ref-:24_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:24-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hauber2014_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hauber2014-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> They measure about 58&#160;mm (2.3&#160;in) in length and 32&#160;mm (1.3&#160;in) in width.<sup id="cite_ref-Hauber2014_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hauber2014-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> The eggs are laid on alternate days, and <a href="/wiki/Incubation_period" title="Incubation period">incubation</a> begins when the clutch is almost complete.<sup id="cite_ref-Hauber2014_78-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hauber2014-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> Incubation takes 27–28<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>days and <a href="/wiki/Fledge" title="Fledge">fledging</a> takes 50–60<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>days.<sup id="cite_ref-:24_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:24-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> The ducklings are <a href="/wiki/Precocial" class="mw-redirect" title="Precocial">precocial</a> and fully capable of swimming as soon as they hatch.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> However, <a href="/wiki/Imprinting_(psychology)" title="Imprinting (psychology)">filial imprinting</a> compels them to instinctively stay near the mother, not only for warmth and protection but also to learn about and remember their habitat as well as how and where to forage for food.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> When ducklings mature into flight-capable juveniles, they learn about and remember their traditional migratory routes (unless they are born and raised in captivity).In New Zealand, where mallards are <a href="/wiki/Naturalisation_(biology)" title="Naturalisation (biology)">naturalised</a>, the nesting season has been found to be longer, eggs and clutches are larger and nest survival is generally greater compared with mallards in their native range. <sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Drake_mallard_Anas_platyrhynchos_performing_the_grunt-whistle_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Drake_mallard_Anas_platyrhynchos_performing_the_grunt-whistle_2.jpg/220px-Drake_mallard_Anas_platyrhynchos_performing_the_grunt-whistle_2.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Drake_mallard_Anas_platyrhynchos_performing_the_grunt-whistle_2.jpg/330px-Drake_mallard_Anas_platyrhynchos_performing_the_grunt-whistle_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Drake_mallard_Anas_platyrhynchos_performing_the_grunt-whistle_2.jpg/440px-Drake_mallard_Anas_platyrhynchos_performing_the_grunt-whistle_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="533" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Drake_mallard_Anas_platyrhynchos_performing_the_grunt-whistle_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Drake mallard performing the grunt-whistle<sup id="cite_ref-Abraham74_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abraham74-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>During the breeding season, both male and female mallards can become aggressive, driving off competitors to themselves or their mate by charging at them.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Males tend to fight more than females, and attack each other by repeatedly pecking at their rival's chest, ripping out feathers and even skin on rare occasions. Female mallards are also known to carry out 'inciting displays', which encourages other ducks in the flock to begin fighting.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> It is possible that this behaviour allows the female to evaluate the strength of potential partners.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The drakes that end up being left out after the others have paired off with mating partners sometimes target an isolated female duck, even one of a different species, and proceed to chase and peck at her until she weakens, at which point the males take turns copulating with the female.<sup id="cite_ref-:10_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:10-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Lebret (1961) calls this behaviour "Attempted Rape Flight", and <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Cramp" title="Stanley Cramp">Stanley Cramp</a> and K.E.L. Simmons (1977) speak of "rape-intent flights".<sup id="cite_ref-:10_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:10-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Male mallards also occasionally chase other male ducks of a different species, and even each other, in the same way.<sup id="cite_ref-:10_86-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:10-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> In one documented case of "homosexual necrophilia", a male mallard copulated with another male he was chasing after the chased male died upon flying into a glass window.<sup id="cite_ref-:10_86-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:10-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> This paper was awarded an <a href="/wiki/Ig_Nobel_Prize" title="Ig Nobel Prize">Ig Nobel Prize</a> in 2003.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Mallards are opportunistically targeted by <a href="/wiki/Brood_parasite" title="Brood parasite">brood parasites</a>, occasionally having eggs laid in their nests by <a href="/wiki/Redhead_(duck)" class="mw-redirect" title="Redhead (duck)">redheads</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ruddy_duck" title="Ruddy duck">ruddy ducks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lesser_scaup" title="Lesser scaup">lesser scaup</a>, gadwalls, <a href="/wiki/Northern_shoveler" title="Northern shoveler">northern shovelers</a>, northern pintails, <a href="/wiki/Cinnamon_teal" title="Cinnamon teal">cinnamon teal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Common_goldeneye" title="Common goldeneye">common goldeneyes</a>, and other mallards.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> These eggs are generally accepted when they resemble the eggs of the host mallard, but the hen may attempt to eject them or even abandon the nest if parasitism occurs during egg laying.<sup id="cite_ref-BNA_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BNA-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Predators_and_threats">Predators and threats</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mallard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Predators and threats">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Mallards of all ages (but especially young ones) and in all locations must contend with a wide diversity of predators including <a href="/wiki/Raptor_birds" class="mw-redirect" title="Raptor birds">raptors</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mustelidae" title="Mustelidae">mustelids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Corvid" class="mw-redirect" title="Corvid">corvids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Snakes" class="mw-redirect" title="Snakes">snakes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Raccoon" title="Raccoon">raccoons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Opossum" title="Opossum">opossums</a>, <a href="/wiki/Skunk" title="Skunk">skunks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turtle" title="Turtle">turtles</a>, large fish, <a href="/wiki/Felidae" title="Felidae">felids</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Canidae" title="Canidae">canids</a>, including domesticated ones.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> The most prolific natural predators of adult mallards are <a href="/wiki/Red_fox" title="Red fox">red foxes</a> (which most often pick off brooding females) and the faster or larger birds of prey, e.g. <a href="/wiki/Peregrine_falcon" title="Peregrine falcon">peregrine falcons</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Aquila_(genus)" title="Aquila (genus)">Aquila</a></i> eagles, or <i><a href="/wiki/Haliaeetus" class="mw-redirect" title="Haliaeetus">Haliaeetus</a></i> eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-NPWRC_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NPWRC-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> In North America, adult mallards face no fewer than 15 species of birds of prey, from <a href="/wiki/Northern_harrier" title="Northern harrier">northern harriers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Short-eared_owl" title="Short-eared owl">short-eared owls</a> (both smaller than a mallard) to huge <a href="/wiki/Bald_eagle" title="Bald eagle">bald</a> and <a href="/wiki/Golden_eagle" title="Golden eagle">golden eagles</a>, and about a dozen species of mammalian predator, not counting several more avian and mammalian predators who threaten eggs and nestlings.<sup id="cite_ref-BNA_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BNA-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Mallards are also preyed upon by other waterside <a href="/wiki/Apex_predator" title="Apex predator">apex predators</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Grey_heron" title="Grey heron">grey heron</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/European_herring_gull" title="European herring gull">European herring gull</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Wels_catfish" title="Wels catfish">wels catfish</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Northern_pike" title="Northern pike">northern pike</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Crows (<i><a href="/wiki/Corvus_(genus)" class="mw-redirect" title="Corvus (genus)">Corvus</a></i><span class="nowrap">&#160;</span><abbr title="species">spp.</abbr>) are also known to kill ducklings and adults on occasion.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Also, mallards may be attacked by larger anseriformes such as <a href="/wiki/Swan" title="Swan">swans</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Cygnus_(genus)" class="mw-redirect" title="Cygnus (genus)">Cygnus</a></i><span class="nowrap">&#160;</span><abbr title="species">spp.</abbr>) and <a href="/wiki/Geese" class="mw-redirect" title="Geese">geese</a> during the breeding season, and are frequently driven off by these birds over territorial disputes. <a href="/wiki/Mute_swan" title="Mute swan">Mute swans</a> (<i>Cygnus olor</i>) have been known to attack or even kill mallards if they feel that the ducks pose a threat to their offspring.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The predation-avoidance behavior of sleeping with one eye open, allowing one brain hemisphere to remain aware while the other half sleeps, was first demonstrated in mallards, although it is believed to be widespread among birds in general.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Status_and_conservation">Status and conservation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mallard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Status and conservation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Deiker_Jagdbare_Tiere_1093218.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Deiker_Jagdbare_Tiere_1093218.jpg/220px-Deiker_Jagdbare_Tiere_1093218.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="192" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Deiker_Jagdbare_Tiere_1093218.jpg/330px-Deiker_Jagdbare_Tiere_1093218.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Deiker_Jagdbare_Tiere_1093218.jpg/440px-Deiker_Jagdbare_Tiere_1093218.jpg 2x" data-file-width="885" data-file-height="771" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Deiker_Jagdbare_Tiere_1093218.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illustration by Carl Friedrich Deiker (1875)</div></div></div>

type=&quot;video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp9, opus&amp;quot;&quot; data-title=&quot;VP9 (360P)&quot; data-shorttitle=&quot;VP9 360P&quot; data-transcodekey=&quot;360p.vp9.webm&quot; data-width=&quot;540&quot; data-height=&quot;360&quot; data-bandwidth=&quot;542416&quot; data-framerate=&quot;29.97002997003&quot;/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;"><img alt="File:Mallards pond 2009.ogv" style="width:220px;height:147px" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Mallards_pond_2009.ogv/220px-seek%3D1-Mallards_pond_2009.ogv.jpg" /><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4a/Mallards_pond_2009.ogv" title="Play media" target="new"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mallards_pond_2009.ogv" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Several drakes swim in a pond.</div></div></div>
<p>Since 1998, the mallard has been rated as a species of <a href="/wiki/Least_concern" class="mw-redirect" title="Least concern">least concern</a> on the <a href="/wiki/IUCN_Red_List_of_Endangered_Species" class="mw-redirect" title="IUCN Red List of Endangered Species">IUCN Red List of Endangered Species</a>. This is because it has a large range–more than 20,000,000&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (7,700,000&#160;mi<sup>2</sup>)<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>–and because its population is increasing, rather than declining by 30% over ten years or three generations and thus is not warranted a <a href="/wiki/Vulnerable_species" title="Vulnerable species">vulnerable</a> rating. Also, the population size of the mallard is very large.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Unlike many waterfowl, mallards have benefited from human alterations to the world&#160;&#8211;&#32;so much so that they are now considered an <a href="/wiki/Invasive_species" title="Invasive species">invasive species</a> in some regions.<sup id="cite_ref-:8_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:8-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> They are a common sight in urban parks, lakes, ponds, and other human-made water features in the regions they inhabit, and are often tolerated or encouraged in human habitat due to their placid nature towards humans and their beautiful and iridescent colours.<sup id="cite_ref-:7_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> While most are not <a href="/wiki/Domesticated" class="mw-redirect" title="Domesticated">domesticated</a>, mallards are so successful at coexisting in human regions that the main conservation risk they pose comes from the loss of genetic diversity among a region's traditional ducks once humans and mallards colonise an area. Mallards are very adaptable, being able to live and even thrive in urban areas which may have supported more localised, sensitive species of waterfowl before development.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> The release of <a href="/wiki/Feral" title="Feral">feral</a> mallards in areas where they are not native sometimes creates problems through interbreeding with indigenous <a href="/wiki/Waterfowl" class="mw-redirect" title="Waterfowl">waterfowl</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:8_99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:8-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:9_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:9-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> These non-migratory mallards interbreed with indigenous wild ducks from local populations of closely related species through <a href="/wiki/Genetic_pollution" title="Genetic pollution">genetic pollution</a> by producing fertile offspring.<sup id="cite_ref-:9_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:9-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> Complete hybridisation of various species of wild duck gene pools could result in the extinction of many indigenous waterfowl.<sup id="cite_ref-:9_101-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:9-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> The wild mallard itself is the ancestor of most domestic ducks, and its naturally evolved wild gene pool gets genetically polluted in turn by the domesticated and feral populations.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Over time, a continuum of hybrids ranging between almost typical examples of either species develop; the <a href="/wiki/Speciation" title="Speciation">speciation</a> process is beginning to reverse itself.<sup id="cite_ref-Rhymer06_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rhymer06-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> This has created <a href="/wiki/Conservation_biology" title="Conservation biology">conservation</a> concerns for relatives of the mallard, such as the <a href="/wiki/Hawaiian_duck" title="Hawaiian duck">Hawaiian duck</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Rhymer96_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rhymer96-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Griffin_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Griffin-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> the New Zealand grey duck (<i>A.<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>s. superciliosa)</i> subspecies of the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_black_duck" title="Pacific black duck">Pacific black duck</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Rhymer96_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rhymer96-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Williams_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> the American black duck,<sup id="cite_ref-Avise_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Avise-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mank_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mank-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Mottled_duck" title="Mottled duck">mottled duck</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-McCracken_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McCracken-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Meller%27s_duck" title="Meller&#39;s duck">Meller's duck</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Young_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Young-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Yellow-billed_duck" title="Yellow-billed duck">yellow-billed duck</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Rhymer06_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rhymer06-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Mexican_duck" title="Mexican duck">Mexican duck</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Rhymer96_103-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rhymer96-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-McCracken_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McCracken-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> in the latter case even leading to a dispute as to whether these birds should be considered a species<sup id="cite_ref-AOU_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AOU-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> (and thus entitled to more conservation research and funding) or included in the mallard species. Ecological changes and hunting have also led to a decline of local species; for example, the New Zealand grey duck population declined drastically due to overhunting in the mid-20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Williams_105-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> Hybrid offspring of Hawaiian ducks seem to be less well adapted to native habitat, and using them in re-introduction projects apparently reduces success.<sup id="cite_ref-Rhymer96_103-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rhymer96-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kirby_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kirby-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> In summary, the problems of mallards "hybridising away" relatives is more a consequence of local ducks declining than of mallards spreading; <a href="/wiki/Allopatric_speciation" title="Allopatric speciation">allopatric speciation</a> and isolating behaviour have produced today's diversity of mallard-like ducks despite the fact that, in most, if not all, of these populations, hybridisation must have occurred to some extent.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Invasiveness">Invasiveness</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mallard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Invasiveness">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Anas_platyrhynchos_oustaleti_last_male.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Anas_platyrhynchos_oustaleti_last_male.jpg/220px-Anas_platyrhynchos_oustaleti_last_male.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="164" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1a/Anas_platyrhynchos_oustaleti_last_male.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="260" data-file-height="194" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Anas_platyrhynchos_oustaleti_last_male.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The last male <a href="/wiki/Mariana_mallard" title="Mariana mallard">Mariana mallard</a></div></div></div> <div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chilling_Duck.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Chilling_Duck.jpg/220px-Chilling_Duck.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Chilling_Duck.jpg/330px-Chilling_Duck.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Chilling_Duck.jpg/440px-Chilling_Duck.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4896" data-file-height="3672" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chilling_Duck.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Male mallard in pond</div></div></div><p> Mallards are causing severe "genetic pollution" to South Africa's <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a> by breeding with endemic ducks<sup id="cite_ref-krugerpark.co.za_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-krugerpark.co.za-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> even though the <i><a href="/wiki/Agreement_on_the_Conservation_of_African-Eurasian_Migratory_Waterbirds" title="Agreement on the Conservation of African-Eurasian Migratory Waterbirds">Agreement on the Conservation of African-Eurasian Migratory Waterbirds</a> – </i>an agreement to protect the local waterfowl populations – applies to the mallard as well as other ducks.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> The hybrids of mallards and the yellow-billed duck are fertile, capable of producing hybrid offspring.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> If this continues, only hybrids occur and in the long term result in the extinction of various indigenous waterfowl.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_115-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> The mallard duck can cross breed with 63&#160;other species, posing a severe threat to indigenous waterfowl's genetic integrity.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> Mallards and their hybrids compete with indigenous birds for resources, including nest sites, roosting sites, and food.<sup id="cite_ref-krugerpark.co.za_113-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-krugerpark.co.za-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Availability of mallards, mallard ducklings, and fertilised mallard eggs for public sale and private ownership, either as poultry or as pets, is currently legal in the United States except for the state of <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a>, which has currently banned domestic ownership of mallards. This is to prevent hybridisation with the native <a href="/wiki/Mottled_duck" title="Mottled duck">mottled duck</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-myfwc2_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-myfwc2-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The mallard is considered an invasive species in New Zealand,<sup id="cite_ref-cramp_22-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:<span>505</span></sup> where it competes with the local New Zealand grey duck, which was overhunted in the past. There, and elsewhere, mallards are spreading with increasing <a href="/wiki/Urbanisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Urbanisation">urbanisation</a> and hybridising with local relatives.<sup id="cite_ref-Rhymer96_103-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rhymer96-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Eastern or <a href="/wiki/Chinese_spot-billed_duck" class="mw-redirect" title="Chinese spot-billed duck">Chinese spot-billed duck</a> is currently introgressing into the mallard populations of the <a href="/wiki/Primorsky_Krai" title="Primorsky Krai">Primorsky Krai</a>, possibly due to <a href="/wiki/Habitat_(ecology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Habitat (ecology)">habitat</a> changes from <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" class="mw-redirect" title="Global warming">global warming</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Kulikova_et_al._2004_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kulikova_et_al._2004-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> The Mariana mallard was a resident <a href="/wiki/Allopatric" class="mw-redirect" title="Allopatric">allopatric</a> population – in most respects a good species – apparently initially derived from mallard-Pacific black duck hybrids;<sup id="cite_ref-Yamashina_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yamashina-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> unfortunately, it became <a href="/wiki/Extinct" class="mw-redirect" title="Extinct">extinct</a> in the late twentieth century.<sup id="cite_ref-MadgeWildfowl_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MadgeWildfowl-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Laysan_duck" title="Laysan duck">Laysan duck</a> is an insular relative of the mallard, with a very small and fluctuating population.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Mallards sometimes arrive on its island home during migration, and can be expected to occasionally have remained and hybridised with Laysan ducks as long as these species have existed.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> However, these hybrids are less well adapted to the peculiar ecological conditions of <a href="/wiki/Laysan_Island" class="mw-redirect" title="Laysan Island">Laysan Island</a> than the local ducks, and thus have lower <a href="/wiki/Fitness_(biology)" title="Fitness (biology)">fitness</a>. Laysan ducks were found throughout the Hawaiian archipelago before 400<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>CE, after which they suffered a rapid decline during the Polynesian colonisation.<sup id="cite_ref-:21_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:21-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> Now, their range includes only Laysan Island.<sup id="cite_ref-:21_122-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:21-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> It is one of the successfully translocated birds, after having become nearly extinct in the early twentieth century.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_with_humans">Relationship with humans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mallard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Relationship with humans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Domestic_duck" title="Domestic duck">Domestic duck</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Domestication">Domestication</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mallard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Domestication">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Mallards have often been ubiquitous in their regions among the ponds, rivers, and streams of human parks, farms, and other human-made waterways&#160;&#8211;&#32;even to the point of visiting water features in human <a href="/wiki/Courtyard" title="Courtyard">courtyards</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nature_Mort_-_Hen_and_Drake_Mallard.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Nature_Mort_-_Hen_and_Drake_Mallard.jpg/220px-Nature_Mort_-_Hen_and_Drake_Mallard.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="168" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Nature_Mort_-_Hen_and_Drake_Mallard.jpg/330px-Nature_Mort_-_Hen_and_Drake_Mallard.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Nature_Mort_-_Hen_and_Drake_Mallard.jpg/440px-Nature_Mort_-_Hen_and_Drake_Mallard.jpg 2x" data-file-width="959" data-file-height="731" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nature_Mort_-_Hen_and_Drake_Mallard.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/George_Hetzel" title="George Hetzel">George Hetzel</a>, Mallard <a href="/wiki/Still_life" title="Still life">still life</a> painting, 1883–1884</div></div></div>
<p>Mallards have had a long relationship with humans. Almost all domestic duck breeds derive from the mallard, with the exception of a few <a href="/wiki/Muscovy_duck" title="Muscovy duck">Muscovy</a> breeds.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> Mallards are generally <a href="/wiki/Monogamy" title="Monogamy">monogamous</a> while domestic ducks are mostly <a href="/wiki/Polygamy" title="Polygamy">polygamous</a>. Domestic ducks have no territorial behaviour and are less aggressive than mallards.<sup id="cite_ref-:23_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:23-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> Domestic ducks are mostly kept for meat; their eggs are also eaten, and have a strong flavor.<sup id="cite_ref-:23_126-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:23-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> They were first domesticated in <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a> at least 4,000 years ago, during the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic Age</a>, and were also farmed by the <a href="/wiki/Demography_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="Demography of the Roman Empire">Romans</a> in Europe, and the <a href="/wiki/Malays_(ethnic_group)" title="Malays (ethnic group)">Malays</a> in <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> As the domestic duck and the mallard are the same species as each other, It is common for mallards to mate with domestic ducks and produce hybrid offspring that are fully fertile.<sup id="cite_ref-:22_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:22-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> Because of this, mallards have been found to be contaminated with the genes of the domestic duck.<sup id="cite_ref-:22_128-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:22-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>While the keeping of domestic breeds is more popular, pure-bred mallards are sometimes kept for eggs and meat,<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> although they may require wing clipping to restrict flying, or training to navigate and fly home.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hunting">Hunting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mallard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Hunting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Mallards are one of the most common varieties of ducks hunted as a sport due to the large population size. The ideal location for hunting mallards is considered to be where the water level is somewhat shallow where the birds can be found foraging for food.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> Hunting mallards might cause the population to decline in some places, at some times, and with some populations.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> In certain countries, the mallard may be legally shot but is protected under national acts and policies. For example, in the United Kingdom, the mallard is protected under the <a href="/wiki/Wildlife_and_Countryside_Act_1981" title="Wildlife and Countryside Act 1981">Wildlife and Countryside Act 1981</a>, which restricts certain hunting methods or taking or killing mallards.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="As_food">As food</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mallard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: As food">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Since ancient times, the mallard has been eaten as food. The wild mallard was eaten in <a href="/wiki/Neolithic_Greece" title="Neolithic Greece">Neolithic Greece</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> Usually, only the breast and thigh meat is eaten.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> It does not need to be <a href="/wiki/Hanging_(meat)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hanging (meat)">hung</a> before preparation, and is often braised or roasted, sometimes flavoured with <a href="/wiki/Bitter_orange" title="Bitter orange">bitter orange</a> or with <a href="/wiki/Port_(wine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Port (wine)">port</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
